	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Afghanistan</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>

</p>

Empire">Durrani Empire</a></span> </div></th><td>October 1747</td></tr><tr class="mergedrow"><th scope="row"><div style="text-indent:-0.9em;margin-left:1.2em;font-weight:normal;">•&#160;<span June 1926</td></tr><tr class="mergedrow"><th scope="row"><div style="text-indent:-0.9em;margin-left:1.2em;font-weight:normal;">•&#160;<span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Afghanistan" class="mergedrow"><th scope="row"><div style="text-indent:-0.9em;margin-left:1.2em;font-weight:normal;">•&#160;<a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Afghanistan" title="Constitution of Afghanistan">Current constitution</a> </div></th><td>26 January 2004</td></tr><tr style="display:none"><td colspan="2">
</td></tr><tr class="mergedtoprow"><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;text-align:left;"><a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Afghanistan" title="Geography of Afghanistan">Area </a></th></tr><tr href="/wiki/.af" title=".af">.af</a> <a href="/wiki/%D8%A7%D9%81%D8%BA%D8%A7%D9%86%D8%B3%D8%AA%D8%A7%D9%86." class="mw-redirect" title="افغانستان.">افغانستان.</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Afghanistan</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/æ/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;bad&#39;">æ</span><span title="&#39;f&#39; in &#39;find&#39;">f</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="/ɡ/: &#39;g&#39; in &#39;guy&#39;">ɡ</span><span title="/æ/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;bad&#39;">æ</span><span title="&#39;n&#39; in &#39;nigh&#39;">n</span><span title="/ɪ/: &#39;i&#39; in &#39;kit&#39;">ɪ</span><span title="&#39;s&#39; in &#39;sigh&#39;">s</span><span title="&#39;t&#39; in &#39;tie&#39;">t</span><span title="/æ/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;bad&#39;">æ</span><span title="&#39;n&#39; in &#39;nigh&#39;">n</span></span>, <wbr /><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/æ/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;bad&#39;">æ</span><span title="&#39;f&#39; in &#39;find&#39;">f</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="/ɡ/: &#39;g&#39; in &#39;guy&#39;">ɡ</span><span title="/ɑː/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;father&#39;">ɑː</span><span title="&#39;n&#39; in &#39;nigh&#39;">n</span><span title="/ɪ/: &#39;i&#39; in &#39;kit&#39;">ɪ</span><span title="&#39;s&#39; in &#39;sigh&#39;">s</span><span title="&#39;t&#39; in &#39;tie&#39;">t</span><span title="/ɑː/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;father&#39;">ɑː</span><span title="&#39;n&#39; in &#39;nigh&#39;">n</span></span>/</a></span>&#32;<span class="nowrap" style="font-size:85%">(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:En-us-Afghanistan.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" decoding="async" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20" /></a></span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/ff/En-us-Afghanistan.ogg" class="internal" title="En-us-Afghanistan.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</span></span>; <a href="/wiki/Pashto" title="Pashto">Pashto</a>/<a href="/wiki/Dari_language" title="Dari language">Dari</a>: <span lang="ps" dir="rtl" title="Pashto language text">افغانستان</span>, Pashto: <i lang="ps-Latn" title="Pashto language text">Afġānistān</i> <small></small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">[avɣɒnisˈtɒn, ab-]</a></span>,<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Dari: <i lang="prs-Latn" title="Dari language text">Afġānestān</i> <small></small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Persian" title="Help:IPA/Persian">[avɣɒnesˈtɒn]</a></span>), officially the <b>Islamic Republic of Afghanistan</b>, is a <a href="/wiki/Landlocked_country" title="Landlocked country">landlocked country</a> located in <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">South</a>-<a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Factbook_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Factbook-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-South_Asia_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-South_Asia-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Afghanistan is bordered by <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a> in the south and east; <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> in the west; <a href="/wiki/Turkmenistan" title="Turkmenistan">Turkmenistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uzbekistan" title="Uzbekistan">Uzbekistan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tajikistan" title="Tajikistan">Tajikistan</a> in the north; and in the far northeast, <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>. Much of its 652,000 square kilometers (252,000&#160;sq&#160;mi) is covered by the <a href="/wiki/Hindu_Kush" title="Hindu Kush">Hindu Kush</a> mountain range at the western end of the <a href="/wiki/Himalayas" title="Himalayas">Himalayas</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERunion20077_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERunion20077-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> separating the <a href="/wiki/Amu_Darya" title="Amu Darya">Amu Darya</a> and <a href="/wiki/Indus_River" title="Indus River">Indus</a> valleys.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Kabul" title="Kabul">Kabul</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Capital_city" title="Capital city">capital</a> and largest city.
</p><p>Human habitation in Afghanistan dates back to the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Paleolithic" title="Middle Paleolithic">Middle Paleolithic</a> Era, and the country's <a href="/wiki/Geostrategy" title="Geostrategy">strategic</a> location along the <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a> connected it to the cultures of the Middle East and other parts of Asia. The land has historically been home to various peoples and has witnessed <a href="/wiki/Invasions_of_Afghanistan" title="Invasions of Afghanistan">numerous military campaigns</a>, including those by <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maurya_Empire" title="Maurya Empire">Mauryas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Islamic_conquest_of_Afghanistan" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamic conquest of Afghanistan">Muslim Arabs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mongol_invasion_of_Khwarezmia_and_Eastern_Iran" class="mw-redirect" title="Mongol invasion of Khwarezmia and Eastern Iran">Mongols</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sikh_empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Sikh empire">Sikhs</a>, <a href="/wiki/First_Anglo-Afghan_War" title="First Anglo-Afghan War">British</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soviet%E2%80%93Afghan_War" title="Soviet–Afghan War">Soviets</a>, and by the <a href="/wiki/War_in_Afghanistan_(2001%E2%80%93present)" title="War in Afghanistan (2001–present)">United States</a> with <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a>-allied countries. It has been called "unconquerable"<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> and nicknamed the "graveyard of empires,"<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> though it has been occupied during several different periods of its history. The land also served as the source from which the <a href="/wiki/Kushan_Empire" title="Kushan Empire">Kushans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hephthalite_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Hephthalite Empire">Hephthalites</a>, <a href="/wiki/Samanid_Empire" title="Samanid Empire">Samanids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saffarid_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Saffarid Empire">Saffarids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ghaznavids" title="Ghaznavids">Ghaznavids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ghorids" class="mw-redirect" title="Ghorids">Ghorids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Khalji_dynasty" title="Khalji dynasty">Khaljis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Empire" title="Mughal Empire">Mughals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hotak_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Hotak Empire">Hotaks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Durrani_dynasty" title="Durrani dynasty">Durranis</a>, and others have risen to form major empires.<sup id="cite_ref-Galvin-PreIslamic_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Galvin-PreIslamic-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The political history of the modern state of Afghanistan began with the Hotak and Durrani dynasties in the 18th century. In the late 19th century, Afghanistan became a <a href="/wiki/Buffer_state" title="Buffer state">buffer state</a> in the "<a href="/wiki/The_Great_Game" title="The Great Game">Great Game</a>" between <a href="/wiki/British_Raj" title="British Raj">British India</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russian Empire</a>. Its border with British India, the <a href="/wiki/Durand_Line" title="Durand Line">Durand Line</a>, was formed in 1893 but it is not recognized by the Afghan government and it has led to strained relations with Pakistan since the latter's independence in 1947. Following the <a href="/wiki/Third_Anglo-Afghan_War" title="Third Anglo-Afghan War">Third Anglo-Afghan War</a> in 1919 the country was free of foreign influence, eventually becoming <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Afghanistan" title="Kingdom of Afghanistan">a monarchy</a> under <a href="/wiki/Amanullah_Khan" title="Amanullah Khan">King Amanullah</a>, until almost 50 years later when <a href="/wiki/Mohammed_Zahir_Shah" title="Mohammed Zahir Shah">Zahir Shah</a> was <a href="/wiki/1973_Afghan_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1973 Afghan coup d&#39;état">overthrown</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Afghanistan" title="Republic of Afghanistan">republic</a> was established. In 1978, after a <a href="/wiki/Saur_Revolution" title="Saur Revolution">second coup</a> Afghanistan first <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_Afghanistan" title="Democratic Republic of Afghanistan">became a socialist state</a> and then a <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> protectorate. This evoked the <a href="/wiki/Soviet%E2%80%93Afghan_War" title="Soviet–Afghan War">Soviet–Afghan War</a> in the 1980s against <a href="/wiki/Mujahideen#Afghanistan" title="Mujahideen">mujahideen</a> rebels. By 1996 most of Afghanistan was captured by the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_fundamentalism" title="Islamic fundamentalism">Islamic fundamentalist</a> group the <a href="/wiki/Taliban" title="Taliban">Taliban</a>, who ruled most of the country as a <a href="/wiki/Totalitarian" class="mw-redirect" title="Totalitarian">totalitarian</a> regime <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Emirate_of_Afghanistan" title="Islamic Emirate of Afghanistan">for over five years</a>. The Taliban were <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Tora_Bora" title="Battle of Tora Bora">forcibly removed</a> by the <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a>-led <a href="/wiki/International_Security_Assistance_Force" title="International Security Assistance Force">coalition</a>, and a new democratically-elected government political structure was formed, but they still control a significant portion of the country.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Afghanistan is a <a href="/wiki/Unitary_state" title="Unitary state">unitary</a> <a href="/wiki/Presidential_system" title="Presidential system">presidential</a> <a href="/wiki/Islamic_republic" title="Islamic republic">Islamic republic</a> with a population of 32 million, mostly composed of ethnic <a href="/wiki/Pashtuns" title="Pashtuns">Pashtuns</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tajiks" title="Tajiks">Tajiks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hazaras" title="Hazaras">Hazaras</a> and <a href="/wiki/Uzbeks" title="Uzbeks">Uzbeks</a>. The country has high levels of <a href="/wiki/War_in_Afghanistan_(2001%E2%80%93present)" title="War in Afghanistan (2001–present)">terrorism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Poverty_in_Afghanistan" title="Poverty in Afghanistan">poverty</a>, <a href="/wiki/Child_malnutrition" class="mw-redirect" title="Child malnutrition">child malnutrition</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Corruption_in_Afghanistan" title="Corruption in Afghanistan">corruption</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> is the <a href="/wiki/State_religion" title="State religion">state religion</a> of Afghanistan. It is a member of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Organisation_of_Islamic_Cooperation" title="Organisation of Islamic Cooperation">Organisation of Islamic Cooperation</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Group_of_77" title="Group of 77">Group of 77</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Economic_Cooperation_Organization" title="Economic Cooperation Organization">Economic Cooperation Organization</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Non-Aligned_Movement" title="Non-Aligned Movement">Non-Aligned Movement</a>. <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Afghanistan" title="Economy of Afghanistan">Afghanistan's economy</a> is the world's 96th largest, with a <a href="/wiki/Gross_domestic_product" title="Gross domestic product">GDP</a> of $72.9 billion by <a href="/wiki/Purchasing_power_parity" title="Purchasing power parity">purchasing power parity</a>; the country fares much worse in terms of per-capita GDP (PPP), ranking 169th out of 186 countries as of 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-imf2_7-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-imf2-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>
<ul>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#Etymology"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span class="toctext">Etymology</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-2"><a href="#History"><span class="tocnumber">2</span> <span class="toctext">History</span></a>
<ul>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Name_of_Afghanistan" title="Name of Afghanistan">Name of Afghanistan</a></div>
<p>The name <i>Afghānistān</i> (<a href="/wiki/Pashto_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Pashto language">Pashto</a>: <span lang="ps" dir="rtl">افغانستان</span>&#8206;) is believed to be as old as the <a href="/wiki/Ethnonym" title="Ethnonym">ethnonym</a> <a href="/wiki/Afghan_(ethnonym)" title="Afghan (ethnonym)"><i>Afghan</i></a>, which is documented in the 10th-century <a href="/wiki/Geography" title="Geography">geography</a> book <i><a href="/wiki/Hudud_ul-%27alam" class="mw-redirect" title="Hudud ul-&#39;alam">Hudud ul-'alam</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Root_(linguistics)" title="Root (linguistics)">root</a> name "<a href="/wiki/Afghan" title="Afghan">Afghan</a>" was used <a href="/wiki/History" title="History">historically</a> in reference to a member of the ethnic <a href="/wiki/Pashtuns" title="Pashtuns">Pashtuns</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Afghan_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Afghan-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Suffix" title="Suffix">suffix</a> "<a href="/wiki/-stan" title="-stan">-stan</a>" means "place of" in <a href="/wiki/Persian_Language" class="mw-redirect" title="Persian Language">Persian</a>. Therefore, Afghanistan translates to <i>land of the Afghans</i><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarfield2012159_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarfield2012159-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> or, more specifically in a historical sense, to <i>land of the Pashtuns</i>. However, the modern <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Afghanistan" title="Constitution of Afghanistan">Constitution of Afghanistan</a> states that "[t]he word Afghan shall apply to every <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Afghanistan" title="Demographics of Afghanistan">citizen of Afghanistan</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Afghanistan" title="History of Afghanistan">History of Afghanistan</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ekhtiarodin_(1).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Ekhtiarodin_%281%29.jpg/220px-Ekhtiarodin_%281%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Ekhtiarodin_%281%29.jpg/330px-Ekhtiarodin_%281%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Ekhtiarodin_%281%29.jpg/440px-Ekhtiarodin_%281%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="667" data-file-height="500" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ekhtiarodin_(1).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Citadel_of_Herat" class="mw-redirect" title="Citadel of Herat">Citadel of Herat</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Excavation_(archaeology)" title="Excavation (archaeology)">Excavations</a> of prehistoric sites suggest that humans were living in what is now Afghanistan at least 50,000 years ago, and that farming communities in the area were among the earliest in the world. An important site of early historical activities, many believe that Afghanistan compares to <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> in terms of the historical value of its archaeological sites.<sup id="cite_ref-JFS_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JFS-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The country sits at a unique nexus point where numerous civilizations have interacted and often fought. It has been home to various peoples through the ages, among them the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Iranian_peoples" class="mw-redirect" title="Ancient Iranian peoples">ancient Iranian peoples</a> who established the dominant role of <a href="/wiki/Indo-Iranian_languages" title="Indo-Iranian languages">Indo-Iranian languages</a> in the region. At multiple points, the land has been incorporated within vast regional empires, among them the <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Achaemenid Empire</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Macedonian Empire</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Maurya_Empire" title="Maurya Empire">Indian Maurya Empire</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_conquest_of_Afghanistan" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamic conquest of Afghanistan">Islamic Empire</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Many empires and kingdoms have also risen to power in Afghanistan, such as the <a href="/wiki/Greco-Bactrian_Kingdom" title="Greco-Bactrian Kingdom">Greco-Bactrians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kushan_Empire" title="Kushan Empire">Kushans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hephthalites" title="Hephthalites">Hephthalites</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kabul_Shahi" class="mw-redirect" title="Kabul Shahi">Kabul Shahis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saffarid_Dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Saffarid Dynasty">Saffarids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Samanids" class="mw-redirect" title="Samanids">Samanids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ghaznavids" title="Ghaznavids">Ghaznavids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ghurid_Dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Ghurid Dynasty">Ghurids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Khalji_dynasty" title="Khalji dynasty">Khaljis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kartids" class="mw-redirect" title="Kartids">Kartids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Timurid_dynasty" title="Timurid dynasty">Timurids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Empire" title="Mughal Empire">Mughals</a>, and finally the <a href="/wiki/Hotak_dynasty" title="Hotak dynasty">Hotak</a> and <a href="/wiki/Durrani_dynasty" title="Durrani dynasty">Durrani</a> dynasties that marked the political origins of the modern state.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERunion200744-49_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERunion200744-49-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-Islamic_period">Pre-Islamic period</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Pre-Islamic_period_of_Afghanistan" class="mw-redirect" title="Pre-Islamic period of Afghanistan">Pre-Islamic period of Afghanistan</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:AsokaKandahar.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/AsokaKandahar.jpg/220px-AsokaKandahar.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="231" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/AsokaKandahar.jpg/330px-AsokaKandahar.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/AsokaKandahar.jpg/440px-AsokaKandahar.jpg 2x" data-file-width="683" data-file-height="717" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:AsokaKandahar.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bilingual (<a href="/wiki/Koine_Greek" title="Koine Greek">Greek</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aramaic" title="Aramaic">Aramaic</a>) <a href="/wiki/Edicts_of_Ashoka" title="Edicts of Ashoka">edict</a> by Emperor <a href="/wiki/Ashoka" title="Ashoka">Ashoka</a> from the 3rd century BCE discovered in the southern city of <a href="/wiki/Kandahar" title="Kandahar">Kandahar</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Archaeology" title="Archaeology">Archaeological</a> exploration done in the 20th century suggests that the geographical area of Afghanistan has been closely connected by culture and trade with its neighbors to the east, west, and north. Artifacts typical of the <a href="/wiki/Paleolithic" title="Paleolithic">Paleolithic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mesolithic" title="Mesolithic">Mesolithic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Iron_age" class="mw-redirect" title="Iron age">Iron ages</a> have been found in Afghanistan. Urban civilization is believed to have begun as early as 3000 BCE, and the early city of <a href="/wiki/Mundigak" title="Mundigak">Mundigak</a> (near <a href="/wiki/Kandahar" title="Kandahar">Kandahar</a> in the south of the country) may have been a colony of the nearby <a href="/wiki/Indus_Valley_Civilization" class="mw-redirect" title="Indus Valley Civilization">Indus Valley Civilization</a>. More recent findings established that the Indus Valley Civilisation stretched up towards modern-day Afghanistan, making the ancient civilization today part of Pakistan, Afghanistan, and India. In more detail, it extended from what today is northwest <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a> to northwest India and northeast Afghanistan. An Indus Valley site has been found on the <a href="/wiki/Oxus_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Oxus River">Oxus River</a> at <a href="/wiki/Shortugai" title="Shortugai">Shortugai</a> in northern Afghanistan.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Ancient_Indus_pp.1_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Ancient_Indus_pp.1-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> There are several smaller IVC colonies to be found in Afghanistan as well.
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r889293853/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{display:flex;flex-direction:row;clear:left;flex-wrap:wrap;width:100%;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{margin:1px;float:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .theader{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{text-align:left;background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-left{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-right{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-center{text-align:center}@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;max-width:none!important;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{justify-content:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Buddha_of_Bamiyan.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Buddha_of_Bamiyan.jpg/200px-Buddha_of_Bamiyan.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="307" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Buddha_of_Bamiyan.jpg/300px-Buddha_of_Bamiyan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Buddha_of_Bamiyan.jpg/400px-Buddha_of_Bamiyan.jpg 2x" data-file-width="508" data-file-height="780" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">The taller <a href="/wiki/Buddha_of_Bamiyan" class="mw-redirect" title="Buddha of Bamiyan">Buddha of Bamiyan</a>. <a href="/wiki/Buddhism_in_Afghanistan" title="Buddhism in Afghanistan">Buddhism</a> was widespread before the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_conquest_of_Afghanistan" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamic conquest of Afghanistan">Islamic conquest of Afghanistan</a>.</div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:BamyanBuddha_Smaller_1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/BamyanBuddha_Smaller_1.jpg/200px-BamyanBuddha_Smaller_1.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="298" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/BamyanBuddha_Smaller_1.jpg/300px-BamyanBuddha_Smaller_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/BamyanBuddha_Smaller_1.jpg/400px-BamyanBuddha_Smaller_1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2080" data-file-height="3095" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">The smaller Buddha.</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>After 2000 BCE, successive waves of semi-nomadic people from Central Asia began moving south into Afghanistan; among them were many <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European-speaking</a> <a href="/wiki/Indo-Iranians" title="Indo-Iranians">Indo-Iranians</a>. These tribes later migrated further into South Asia, Western Asia, and toward Europe via the area north of the <a href="/wiki/Caspian_Sea" title="Caspian Sea">Caspian Sea</a>. The region at the time was referred to as <a href="/wiki/Ariana" title="Ariana">Ariana</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-JFS_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JFS-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The religion <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrianism" title="Zoroastrianism">Zoroastrianism</a> is believed by some to have originated in what is now Afghanistan between 1800 and 800 BCE, as its founder <a href="/wiki/Zoroaster" title="Zoroaster">Zoroaster</a> is thought to have lived and died in <a href="/wiki/Balkh" title="Balkh">Balkh</a>. Ancient <a href="/wiki/Iranian_languages" title="Iranian languages">Eastern Iranian languages</a> may have been spoken in the region around the time of the rise of Zoroastrianism. By the middle of the 6th century BCE, the Achaemenids overthrew the <a href="/wiki/Medes" title="Medes">Medes</a> and incorporated <a href="/wiki/Arachosia" title="Arachosia">Arachosia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aria_(satrapy)" class="mw-redirect" title="Aria (satrapy)">Aria</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bactria" title="Bactria">Bactria</a> within its eastern boundaries. An <a href="/wiki/Epigraphy" title="Epigraphy">inscription</a> on the tombstone of <a href="/wiki/Darius_I_of_Persia" class="mw-redirect" title="Darius I of Persia">Darius I of Persia</a> mentions the <a href="/wiki/Kabulistan" title="Kabulistan">Kabul Valley</a> in a list of the 29 countries that he had conquered.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a> and his Macedonian forces arrived in Afghanistan in 330 BCE after defeating <a href="/wiki/Darius_III_of_Persia" class="mw-redirect" title="Darius III of Persia">Darius III of Persia</a> a year earlier in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Gaugamela" title="Battle of Gaugamela">Battle of Gaugamela</a>. Following Alexander's brief occupation, the successor state of the <a href="/wiki/Seleucid_Empire" title="Seleucid Empire">Seleucid Empire</a> controlled the region until 305 BCE when they gave much of it to the <a href="/wiki/Maurya_Empire" title="Maurya Empire">Maurya Empire</a> as part of an alliance treaty. The Mauryans controlled the area south of the <a href="/wiki/Hindu_Kush" title="Hindu Kush">Hindu Kush</a> until they were overthrown in about 185 BCE. Their decline began 60 years after <a href="/wiki/Ashoka" title="Ashoka">Ashoka</a>'s rule ended, leading to the <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_civilization" class="mw-redirect" title="Hellenistic civilization">Hellenistic</a> reconquest by the <a href="/wiki/Greco-Bactrians" class="mw-redirect" title="Greco-Bactrians">Greco-Bactrians</a>. Much of it soon broke away from them and became part of the <a href="/wiki/Indo-Greek_Kingdom" title="Indo-Greek Kingdom">Indo-Greek Kingdom</a>. They were defeated and expelled by the <a href="/wiki/Indo-Scythians" title="Indo-Scythians">Indo-Scythians</a> in the late 2nd century BCE.<sup id="cite_ref-LoC-pdf_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LoC-pdf-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERunion200744_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERunion200744-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During the first century BCE, the <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">Parthian Empire</a> subjugated the region but lost it to their <a href="/wiki/Indo-Parthian" class="mw-redirect" title="Indo-Parthian">Indo-Parthian</a> vassals. In the mid-to-late first century CE the vast <a href="/wiki/Kushan_Empire" title="Kushan Empire">Kushan Empire</a>, centered in Afghanistan, became great patrons of Buddhist culture, making <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> flourish throughout the region. The Kushans were overthrown by the <a href="/wiki/Sassanid_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Sassanid Empire">Sassanids</a> in the 3rd century CE, though the <a href="/wiki/Indo-Sassanids" class="mw-redirect" title="Indo-Sassanids">Indo-Sassanids</a> continued to rule at least parts of the region. They were followed by the <a href="/wiki/Kidarite" class="mw-redirect" title="Kidarite">Kidarite</a> who, in turn, were replaced by the <a href="/wiki/Hephthalites" title="Hephthalites">Hephthalites</a>. By the 6th century CE, the successors to the Kushans and Hepthalites established occupied the <a href="/wiki/Gandhara" title="Gandhara">Gandhara</a> region and became Indianized and adopted <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a>. They were replaced by the <a href="/wiki/Turk_Shahi" title="Turk Shahi">Turk Shahi</a> in the 7th century. The Buddhist Turk Shahi of Kabul was replaced by a Hindu dynasty before the Saffarids conquered the area in 870, this Hindu dynasty was called <a href="/wiki/Hindu_Shahi" title="Hindu Shahi">Hindu Shahi</a>. Much of the northeastern and southern areas of the country remained dominated by <a href="/wiki/Buddhist" class="mw-redirect" title="Buddhist">Buddhist</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hindu" class="mw-redirect" title="Hindu">Hindu</a> culture.<sup id="cite_ref-Habibi_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Habibi-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Islamization_and_Mongol_invasion">Islamization and Mongol invasion</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Islamic_conquest_of_Afghanistan" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamic conquest of Afghanistan">Islamic conquest of Afghanistan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mongol_invasion_of_Central_Asia" class="mw-redirect" title="Mongol invasion of Central Asia">Mongol invasion of Central Asia</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Herat_Congregational_Mosque_-Afghanistan.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Herat_Congregational_Mosque_-Afghanistan.jpg/220px-Herat_Congregational_Mosque_-Afghanistan.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="90" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Herat_Congregational_Mosque_-Afghanistan.jpg/330px-Herat_Congregational_Mosque_-Afghanistan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Herat_Congregational_Mosque_-Afghanistan.jpg/440px-Herat_Congregational_Mosque_-Afghanistan.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4907" data-file-height="2015" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Herat_Congregational_Mosque_-Afghanistan.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Friday_Mosque_of_Herat" class="mw-redirect" title="Friday Mosque of Herat">Friday Mosque of Herat</a> is one of the oldest mosques in Afghanistan</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mazar-e_sharif_-_Steve_Evans.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Mazar-e_sharif_-_Steve_Evans.jpg/220px-Mazar-e_sharif_-_Steve_Evans.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="158" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Mazar-e_sharif_-_Steve_Evans.jpg/330px-Mazar-e_sharif_-_Steve_Evans.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Mazar-e_sharif_-_Steve_Evans.jpg/440px-Mazar-e_sharif_-_Steve_Evans.jpg 2x" data-file-width="965" data-file-height="695" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mazar-e_sharif_-_Steve_Evans.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Blue_Mosque_(Mazar-i-Sharif)" title="Blue Mosque (Mazar-i-Sharif)">Blue Mosque</a> in Mazar-i-Sharif was built in the 15th century</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Arab" class="mw-redirect" title="Arab">Arab</a> <a href="/wiki/Muslim" class="mw-redirect" title="Muslim">Muslims</a> brought Islam to <a href="/wiki/Herat" title="Herat">Herat</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zaranj" title="Zaranj">Zaranj</a> in 642&#160;CE and began spreading eastward; some of the native inhabitants they encountered accepted it while others revolted. Before Islam was introduced, people of the region were mostly Buddhists and Zoroastrians, but there were also <a href="/wiki/Surya" title="Surya">Surya</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nana_(Afghan_goddess)" class="mw-redirect" title="Nana (Afghan goddess)">Nana</a> worshipers, <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Jews_in_Afghanistan" title="History of the Jews in Afghanistan">Jews</a>, and others. The <a href="/wiki/Zunbils" title="Zunbils">Zunbils</a> and Kabul Shahi were first conquered in 870&#160;CE by the <a href="/wiki/Saffarid_Dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Saffarid Dynasty">Saffarid</a> Muslims of Zaranj. Later, the <a href="/wiki/Samanids" class="mw-redirect" title="Samanids">Samanids</a> extended their Islamic influence south of the <a href="/wiki/Hindu" class="mw-redirect" title="Hindu">Hindu</a> Kush. It is reported that Muslims and non-Muslims still lived side by side in Kabul before the <a href="/wiki/Ghaznavids" title="Ghaznavids">Ghaznavids</a> rose to power in the 10th century.<sup id="cite_ref-Elliot-2_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Elliot-2-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mustawfi_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mustawfi-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Elliot-3_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Elliot-3-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>By the 11th century, <a href="/wiki/Mahmud_of_Ghazni" title="Mahmud of Ghazni">Mahmud of Ghazni</a> defeated the remaining Hindu rulers and effectively <a href="/wiki/Islamized" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamized">Islamized</a> the wider region,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEwans200222-23_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEwans200222-23-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> with the exception of <a href="/wiki/Kafiristan" title="Kafiristan">Kafiristan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-nuristan.info_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nuristan.info-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Mahmud made <a href="/wiki/Ghazni" title="Ghazni">Ghazni</a> into an important city and patronized intellectuals such as the historian <a href="/wiki/Al-Biruni" title="Al-Biruni">Al-Biruni</a> and the poet <a href="/wiki/Ferdowsi" title="Ferdowsi">Ferdowsi</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Ghaznavids" title="Ghaznavids">Ghaznavid dynasty</a> was overthrown by the <a href="/wiki/Ghurid_Dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Ghurid Dynasty">Ghurids</a>, whose architectural achievements included the remote <a href="/wiki/Minaret_of_Jam" title="Minaret of Jam">Minaret of Jam</a>.  The Ghurids controlled Afghanistan for less than a century before being conquered by the <a href="/wiki/Khwarazmian_dynasty" title="Khwarazmian dynasty">Khwarazmian dynasty</a> in 1215.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEwans200223_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEwans200223-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1219&#160;AD, <a href="/wiki/Genghis_Khan" title="Genghis Khan">Genghis Khan</a> and his <a href="/wiki/Mongol_Empire" title="Mongol Empire">Mongol</a> army overran the region. His troops are said to have annihilated the Khorasanian cities of Herat and Balkh as well as <a href="/wiki/Bamyan,_Afghanistan" class="mw-redirect" title="Bamyan, Afghanistan">Bamyan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> The destruction caused by the Mongols forced many locals to return to an agrarian rural society.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Mongol rule continued with the <a href="/wiki/Ilkhanate" title="Ilkhanate">Ilkhanate</a> in the northwest while the <a href="/wiki/Khalji_dynasty" title="Khalji dynasty">Khalji dynasty</a> administered the Afghan tribal areas south of the Hindu Kush until the invasion of <a href="/wiki/Timur" title="Timur">Timur</a>, who established the <a href="/wiki/Timurid_Empire" title="Timurid Empire">Timurid Empire</a> in 1370.
</p><p>In the early 16th century, <a href="/wiki/Babur" title="Babur">Babur</a> arrived from <a href="/wiki/Fergana" title="Fergana">Fergana</a> and captured Kabul from the <a href="/wiki/Arghun_dynasty" title="Arghun dynasty">Arghun dynasty</a>. In 1526, he invaded <a href="/wiki/Delhi_Sultanate" title="Delhi Sultanate">Delhi</a> in India to replace the <a href="/wiki/Lodi_dynasty" title="Lodi dynasty">Lodi dynasty</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Empire" title="Mughal Empire">Mughal Empire</a>. Between the 16th and 18th century, the <a href="/wiki/Khanate_of_Bukhara" title="Khanate of Bukhara">Khanate of Bukhara</a>, <a href="/wiki/Safavid_dynasty" title="Safavid dynasty">Safavids</a>, and Mughals ruled parts of the territory. Before the 19th century, the northwestern area of Afghanistan was referred to by the regional name <a href="/wiki/Greater_Khorasan" title="Greater Khorasan">Khorasan</a>. Two of the four capitals of Khorasan (<a href="/wiki/Herat" title="Herat">Herat</a> and <a href="/wiki/Balkh" title="Balkh">Balkh</a>) are now located in Afghanistan, while the regions of <a href="/wiki/Kandahar_Province" title="Kandahar Province">Kandahar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zabul_Province" title="Zabul Province">Zabulistan</a>, Ghazni, Kabulistan, and <a href="/wiki/Name_of_Afghanistan" title="Name of Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a> formed the <a href="/wiki/Frontier" title="Frontier">frontier</a> between Khorasan and <a href="/wiki/Hindustan" title="Hindustan">Hindustan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-EI_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EI-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Routledge_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Routledge-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Firishta_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Firishta-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hotak_dynasty_and_Durrani_Empire">Hotak dynasty and Durrani Empire</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Hotak_dynasty" title="Hotak dynasty">Hotak dynasty</a> and <a href="/wiki/Durrani_Empire" title="Durrani Empire">Durrani Empire</a></div>
<p>In 1709, <a href="/wiki/Mirwais_Hotak" title="Mirwais Hotak">Mirwais Hotak</a>, a local <a href="/wiki/Ghilji" title="Ghilji">Ghilzai</a> tribal leader, successfully rebelled against the Safavids. He defeated <a href="/wiki/George_XI_of_Kartli" title="George XI of Kartli">Gurgin Khan</a> and made Afghanistan independent.<sup id="cite_ref-Browne_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Browne-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> Mirwais died of natural causes in 1715 and was succeeded by his brother <a href="/wiki/Abdul_Aziz_Hotak" title="Abdul Aziz Hotak">Abdul Aziz</a>, who was soon killed by Mirwais' son <a href="/wiki/Mahmud_Hotak" title="Mahmud Hotak">Mahmud</a> for <a href="/wiki/Treason" title="Treason">treason</a>. Mahmud led the Afghan army in 1722 to the Persian capital of <a href="/wiki/Isfahan" title="Isfahan">Isfahan</a>, captured the city after the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Gulnabad" title="Battle of Gulnabad">Battle of Gulnabad</a> and proclaimed himself King of Persia.<sup id="cite_ref-Browne_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Browne-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> The Afghan dynasty was ousted from Persia by <a href="/wiki/Nader_Shah" title="Nader Shah">Nader Shah</a> after the 1729 <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Damghan_(1729)" title="Battle of Damghan (1729)">Battle of Damghan</a>.
</p><p>In 1738, Nader Shah and his <a href="/wiki/Afsharid_dynasty" title="Afsharid dynasty">forces</a> captured Kandahar, the last Hotak stronghold, from Shah <a href="/wiki/Hussain_Hotak" title="Hussain Hotak">Hussain Hotak</a>, at which point the incarcerated 16-year-old <a href="/wiki/Ahmad_Shah_Durrani" title="Ahmad Shah Durrani">Ahmad Shah Durrani</a> was freed and made the commander of an Afghan regiment. Soon after the Persian and Afghan forces <a href="/wiki/Nader_Shah%27s_invasion_of_India" class="mw-redirect" title="Nader Shah&#39;s invasion of India">invaded India</a>. By 1747, the <a href="/wiki/Afghans" class="mw-redirect" title="Afghans">Afghans</a> chose Durrani as their <a href="/wiki/Head_of_state" title="Head of state">head of state</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Brit-Durrani_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brit-Durrani-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> Durrani and his Afghan army conquered much of present-day Afghanistan, <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Khorasan_Province" title="Khorasan Province">Khorasan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Quhistan" title="Quhistan">Kohistan</a> provinces of Iran, and Delhi in India.<sup id="cite_ref-Engels_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Engels-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> He defeated the Indian <a href="/wiki/Maratha_Empire" title="Maratha Empire">Maratha Empire</a>, and one of his biggest victories was the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Panipat_(1761)" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of Panipat (1761)">1761 Battle of Panipat</a>.
</p><p>In October 1772, Durrani died of natural causes and was buried at a site now adjacent to the <a href="/wiki/Shrine_of_the_Cloak" title="Shrine of the Cloak">Shrine of the Cloak</a> in Kandahar. He was succeeded by his son, <a href="/wiki/Timur_Shah_Durrani" title="Timur Shah Durrani">Timur Shah</a>, who transferred the capital of Afghanistan from Kandahar to Kabul in 1776. After Timur's death in 1793, the Durrani throne passed down to his son <a href="/wiki/Zaman_Shah_Durrani" title="Zaman Shah Durrani">Zaman Shah</a>, followed by <a href="/wiki/Mahmud_Shah_Durrani" title="Mahmud Shah Durrani">Mahmud Shah</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shuja_Shah_Durrani" class="mw-redirect" title="Shuja Shah Durrani">Shuja Shah</a> and others.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Afghan_foot_soldiers_in_1841.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Afghan_foot_soldiers_in_1841.jpg/220px-Afghan_foot_soldiers_in_1841.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Afghan_foot_soldiers_in_1841.jpg/330px-Afghan_foot_soldiers_in_1841.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Afghan_foot_soldiers_in_1841.jpg/440px-Afghan_foot_soldiers_in_1841.jpg 2x" data-file-width="940" data-file-height="712" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Afghan_foot_soldiers_in_1841.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Afghan tribesmen in 1841, painted by British officer <a href="/wiki/James_Rattray" title="James Rattray">James Rattray</a></div></div></div>
<p>By the early 19th century, the Afghan empire was under threat from the <a href="/wiki/Qajar_dynasty" title="Qajar dynasty">Persians</a> in the west and the <a href="/wiki/Sikh_Empire" title="Sikh Empire">Sikh Empire</a> in the east. Fateh Khan, leader of the <a href="/wiki/Barakzai_dynasty" title="Barakzai dynasty">Barakzai tribe</a>, had installed 21 of his brothers in positions of power throughout the empire. After his death, they rebelled and divided up the provinces of the empire between themselves. During this turbulent period, Afghanistan had many temporary rulers until <a href="/wiki/Dost_Mohammad_Khan" title="Dost Mohammad Khan">Dost Mohammad Khan</a> declared himself emir in 1826.<sup id="cite_ref-Tanner_2009_126_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tanner_2009_126-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Punjab_region" class="mw-redirect" title="Punjab region">Punjab region</a> was lost to <a href="/wiki/Ranjit_Singh" title="Ranjit Singh">Ranjit Singh</a>, who invaded <a href="/wiki/Khyber_Pakhtunkhwa" title="Khyber Pakhtunkhwa">Khyber Pakhtunkhwa</a> and in 1834 captured the city of <a href="/wiki/Peshawar" title="Peshawar">Peshawar</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Nalwa_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nalwa-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> In 1837, during the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Jamrud" title="Battle of Jamrud">Battle of Jamrud</a> near the <a href="/wiki/Khyber_Pass" title="Khyber Pass">Khyber Pass</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wazir_Akbar_Khan" title="Wazir Akbar Khan">Akbar Khan</a> and the Afghan army failed to capture the Jamrud fort from the <a href="/wiki/Sikh_Khalsa_Army" title="Sikh Khalsa Army">Sikh Khalsa Army</a>, but killed Sikh Commander <a href="/wiki/Hari_Singh_Nalwa" title="Hari Singh Nalwa">Hari Singh Nalwa</a>, thus ending the <a href="/wiki/Afghan-Sikh_Wars" class="mw-redirect" title="Afghan-Sikh Wars">Afghan-Sikh Wars</a>. By this time the British were advancing from the east and the <a href="/wiki/First_Anglo-Afghan_War" title="First Anglo-Afghan War">first major conflict</a> during "<a href="/wiki/The_Great_Game" title="The Great Game">The Great Game</a>" was initiated.<sup id="cite_ref-Chahryar_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chahryar-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="British_influence_and_independent_kingdom">British influence and independent kingdom</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/European_influence_in_Afghanistan" title="European influence in Afghanistan">European influence in Afghanistan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Afghan_Civil_War_(1928%E2%80%931929)" title="Afghan Civil War (1928–1929)">Afghan Civil War (1928–1929)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Forces_from_British_India_camping_at_Kandahar.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Forces_from_British_India_camping_at_Kandahar.jpg/220px-Forces_from_British_India_camping_at_Kandahar.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="172" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Forces_from_British_India_camping_at_Kandahar.jpg/330px-Forces_from_British_India_camping_at_Kandahar.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Forces_from_British_India_camping_at_Kandahar.jpg/440px-Forces_from_British_India_camping_at_Kandahar.jpg 2x" data-file-width="881" data-file-height="687" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Forces_from_British_India_camping_at_Kandahar.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/British_Raj" title="British Raj">British</a> and <a href="/wiki/Afghan_National_Army" title="Afghan National Army">allied forces</a> at Kandahar after the 1880 <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Kandahar" title="Battle of Kandahar">Battle of Kandahar</a>, during the <a href="/wiki/Second_Anglo-Afghan_War" title="Second Anglo-Afghan War">Second Anglo-Afghan War</a>. The large defensive wall around the city was removed in the early 1930s by order of <a href="/wiki/Mohammed_Nadir_Shah" title="Mohammed Nadir Shah">King Nadir</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>In 1838, the British marched into Afghanistan and arrested <a href="/wiki/Dost_Mohammad_Khan_(Emir_of_Afghanistan)" class="mw-redirect" title="Dost Mohammad Khan (Emir of Afghanistan)">Dost Mohammad</a>, sent him into exile in India and replaced him with the previous ruler, <a href="/wiki/Shah_Shujah_Durrani" title="Shah Shujah Durrani">Shah Shuja</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ingram1980_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ingram1980-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ingram1984_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ingram1984-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Following an uprising, the <a href="/wiki/1842_retreat_from_Kabul" title="1842 retreat from Kabul">1842 retreat from Kabul</a> of British-Indian forces and the annihilation of <a href="/wiki/William_George_Keith_Elphinstone" title="William George Keith Elphinstone">Elphinstone</a>'s army, and the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Kabul_(1842)" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of Kabul (1842)">Battle of Kabul</a> that led to its recapture, the British placed Dost Mohammad Khan back into power and withdrew their military forces from Afghanistan. In 1878, the <a href="/wiki/Second_Anglo-Afghan_War" title="Second Anglo-Afghan War">Second Anglo-Afghan War</a> was fought over perceived Russian influence, <a href="/wiki/Abdur_Rahman_Khan" title="Abdur Rahman Khan">Abdur Rahman Khan</a> replaced <a href="/wiki/Mohammad_Ayub_Khan_(Emir_of_Afghanistan)" class="mw-redirect" title="Mohammad Ayub Khan (Emir of Afghanistan)">Ayub Khan</a>, and Britain gained control of Afghanistan's <a href="/wiki/Foreign_policy" title="Foreign policy">foreign relations</a> as part of the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Gandamak" title="Treaty of Gandamak">Treaty of Gandamak</a> of 1879. In 1893, <a href="/wiki/Mortimer_Durand" title="Mortimer Durand">Mortimer Durand</a> made Amir Abdur Rahman Khan sign a controversial agreement in which the ethnic <a href="/wiki/Pashtun_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Pashtun people">Pashtun</a> and <a href="/wiki/Baloch_people" title="Baloch people">Baloch</a> territories were divided by the <a href="/wiki/Durand_Line" title="Durand Line">Durand Line</a>. This was a standard <a href="/wiki/Divide_and_rule" title="Divide and rule">divide and rule</a> policy of the British and would lead to strained relations, especially with the later new state of Pakistan. <a href="/wiki/Shia_Islam_in_Afghanistan" title="Shia Islam in Afghanistan">Shia</a>-dominated <a href="/wiki/Hazarajat" title="Hazarajat">Hazarajat</a> and pagan <a href="/wiki/Kafiristan" title="Kafiristan">Kafiristan</a> remained politically independent until being <a href="/wiki/Muslim_conquests_of_Afghanistan" title="Muslim conquests of Afghanistan">conquered</a> by Abdur Rahman Khan in 1891–1896.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Zahir_Shah_of_Afghanistan_in_1930s-cropped.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Zahir_Shah_of_Afghanistan_in_1930s-cropped.jpg/170px-Zahir_Shah_of_Afghanistan_in_1930s-cropped.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="255" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Zahir_Shah_of_Afghanistan_in_1930s-cropped.jpg/255px-Zahir_Shah_of_Afghanistan_in_1930s-cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/ad/Zahir_Shah_of_Afghanistan_in_1930s-cropped.jpg 2x" data-file-width="320" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Zahir_Shah_of_Afghanistan_in_1930s-cropped.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Mohammed_Zahir_Shah" title="Mohammed Zahir Shah">Zahir Shah</a>, the last king of Afghanistan, who reigned from 1933 to 1973.</div></div></div>
<p>After the <a href="/wiki/Third_Anglo-Afghan_War" title="Third Anglo-Afghan War">Third Anglo-Afghan War</a> and the signing of the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Afghan_Treaty_of_1919" title="Anglo-Afghan Treaty of 1919">Treaty of Rawalpindi</a> on 19 August 1919, King <a href="/wiki/Am%C4%81null%C4%81h_Kh%C4%81n" class="mw-redirect" title="Amānullāh Khān">Amanullah Khan</a> declared Afghanistan a <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_state" title="Sovereign state">sovereign</a> and fully <a href="/wiki/Independence" title="Independence">independent state</a>. He moved to end his country's traditional isolation by establishing diplomatic relations with the international community and, following a 1927–28 tour of Europe and <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>, introduced several reforms intended to modernize his nation. A key force behind these reforms was <a href="/wiki/Mahmud_Tarzi" title="Mahmud Tarzi">Mahmud Tarzi</a>, an ardent supporter of the education of women. He fought for Article 68 of Afghanistan's 1923 <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Afghanistan" title="Constitution of Afghanistan">constitution</a>, which made elementary education compulsory. The institution of <a href="/wiki/Slavery" title="Slavery">slavery</a> was abolished in 1923.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-05499,_Berlin,_Besuch_K%C3%B6nig_von_Afghanistan.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-05499%2C_Berlin%2C_Besuch_K%C3%B6nig_von_Afghanistan.jpg/200px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-05499%2C_Berlin%2C_Besuch_K%C3%B6nig_von_Afghanistan.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="257" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-05499%2C_Berlin%2C_Besuch_K%C3%B6nig_von_Afghanistan.jpg/300px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-05499%2C_Berlin%2C_Besuch_K%C3%B6nig_von_Afghanistan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-05499%2C_Berlin%2C_Besuch_K%C3%B6nig_von_Afghanistan.jpg/400px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-05499%2C_Berlin%2C_Besuch_K%C3%B6nig_von_Afghanistan.jpg 2x" data-file-width="536" data-file-height="688" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-05499,_Berlin,_Besuch_K%C3%B6nig_von_Afghanistan.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>King <a href="/wiki/Amanullah_Khan" title="Amanullah Khan">Amanullah Khan</a> and Queen <a href="/wiki/Soraya_Tarzi" title="Soraya Tarzi">Soraya Tarzi</a> on a visit to Berlin in 1928</div></div></div>
<p>Some of the reforms that were put in place, such as the abolition of the traditional <a href="/wiki/Burqa" title="Burqa">burqa</a> for women and the opening of several co-educational schools, quickly alienated many tribal and religious leaders, and this led to the <a href="/wiki/Afghan_Civil_War_(1928%E2%80%931929)" title="Afghan Civil War (1928–1929)">Afghan Civil War (1928–1929)</a>. Faced with the overwhelming armed opposition, Amanullah Khan abdicated in January 1929, and soon after Kabul fell to <a href="/wiki/Saqqawists" title="Saqqawists">Saqqawist</a> forces led by <a href="/wiki/Habibullah_Kalakani" class="mw-redirect" title="Habibullah Kalakani">Habibullah Kalakani</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Prince <a href="/wiki/Mohammed_Nadir_Shah" title="Mohammed Nadir Shah">Mohammed Nadir Shah</a>, Amanullah's cousin, in turn defeated and killed Kalakani in October 1929, and was declared King Nadir Shah.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> He abandoned the reforms of Amanullah Khan in favor of a more gradual approach to modernization but was assassinated in 1933 by <a href="/wiki/Abdul_Khaliq_Hazara_(assassin)" title="Abdul Khaliq Hazara (assassin)">Abdul Khaliq</a>, a fifteen-year-old <a href="/wiki/Hazara_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Hazara people">Hazara</a> student.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Mohammed_Zahir_Shah" title="Mohammed Zahir Shah">Mohammed Zahir Shah</a>, Nadir Shah's 19-year-old son, succeeded to the throne and reigned from 1933 to 1973. Until 1946, Zahir Shah ruled with the assistance of his uncle, who held the post of <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Afghanistan" title="Prime Minister of Afghanistan">Prime Minister</a> and continued the policies of Nadir Shah. Another of Zahir Shah's uncles, <a href="/wiki/Shah_Mahmud_Khan" title="Shah Mahmud Khan">Shah Mahmud Khan</a>, became Prime Minister in 1946 and began an experiment allowing greater political freedom, but reversed the policy when it went further than he expected. He was replaced in 1953 by <a href="/wiki/Mohammed_Daoud_Khan" title="Mohammed Daoud Khan">Mohammed Daoud Khan</a>, the king's cousin and brother-in-law, and a <a href="/wiki/Pashtuns" title="Pashtuns">Pashtun</a> nationalist who sought the creation of a <a href="/wiki/Pashtunistan" title="Pashtunistan">Pashtunistan</a>, leading to highly tense relations with Pakistan.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> During his ten years at the post until 1963, Daoud Khan pressed for social modernization reforms and sought a closer relationship with the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>. Afterward, the <a href="/wiki/1964_Constitution_of_Afghanistan" title="1964 Constitution of Afghanistan">1964 constitution</a> was formed, and the first non-royal Prime Minister was sworn in.<sup id="cite_ref-Eur2002_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eur2002-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>King Zahir Shah, like his father Nadir Shah, had a policy of maintaining national independence while pursuing gradual modernization, creating nationalist feeling, and improving relations with the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>. Close relations with the Muslim states <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Iraq" title="Kingdom of Iraq">Kingdom of Iraq</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pahlavi_dynasty" title="Pahlavi dynasty">Iran/Persia</a> were also pursued, while further international relations were sought by joining the <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations" title="League of Nations">League of Nations</a> in 1934. The 1930s saw the development of roads, infrastructure, the founding of a <a href="/wiki/Da_Afghanistan_Bank" title="Da Afghanistan Bank">national bank</a>, and increased education. Road links in the north played a large part in a growing <a href="/wiki/Cotton" title="Cotton">cotton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Textile" title="Textile">textile</a> industry.<sup id="cite_ref-Eur2002_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eur2002-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> The country built close relationships with the <a href="/wiki/Axis_powers" title="Axis powers">Axis powers</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a> having the largest share in Afghan development at the time.<sup id="cite_ref-Hyman2016_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hyman2016-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> However, Afghanistan remained neutral and was neither a participant in <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> nor aligned with either power bloc in the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a> thereafter. However, it was a beneficiary of the latter rivalry as both the Soviet Union and the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> vied for influence by building Afghanistan's main highways, airports, and other vital infrastructure. On a per capita basis, Afghanistan received more Soviet <a href="/wiki/Development_aid" title="Development aid">development aid</a> than any other country. Afghanistan had, therefore, good relations with both Cold War enemies. In 1973, while the King was on an official overseas visit, Daoud Khan launched a <a href="/wiki/1973_Afghan_coup" class="mw-redirect" title="1973 Afghan coup">bloodless coup</a> and became the first <a href="/wiki/President_of_Afghanistan" title="President of Afghanistan">President of Afghanistan</a>, abolishing the monarchy. In the meantime, <a href="/wiki/Zulfikar_Ali_Bhutto" title="Zulfikar Ali Bhutto">Zulfikar Ali Bhutto</a> got neighboring Pakistan involved in Afghanistan. Some experts suggest that Bhutto paved the way for the April 1978 <a href="/wiki/Saur_Revolution" title="Saur Revolution">Saur Revolution</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Bowersox_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bowersox-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="PDPA_coup_d.27.C3.A9tat_and_Soviet_war"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="PDPA_coup_d'état_and_Soviet_war">PDPA coup d'état and Soviet war</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Saur_Revolution" title="Saur Revolution">Saur Revolution</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soviet%E2%80%93Afghan_War" title="Soviet–Afghan War">Soviet–Afghan War</a>, <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_Afghanistan" title="Democratic Republic of Afghanistan">Democratic Republic of Afghanistan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/History_of_Afghanistan_(1978%E2%80%9392)" title="History of Afghanistan (1978–92)">History of Afghanistan (1978–92)</a></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r889293853/mw-parser-output/.tmulti"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:358px;max-width:358px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Afgan1987_Gardez_UAZ469.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Afgan1987_Gardez_UAZ469.jpg/200px-Afgan1987_Gardez_UAZ469.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="138" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Afgan1987_Gardez_UAZ469.jpg/300px-Afgan1987_Gardez_UAZ469.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Afgan1987_Gardez_UAZ469.jpg/400px-Afgan1987_Gardez_UAZ469.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5328" data-file-height="3663" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Soviet troops in <a href="/wiki/Gardez" title="Gardez">Gardez</a>, Afghanistan in 1987</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:152px;max-width:152px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Jamiat_e-Islami_in_Shultan_Valley_1987_with_Dashaka.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Jamiat_e-Islami_in_Shultan_Valley_1987_with_Dashaka.jpg/150px-Jamiat_e-Islami_in_Shultan_Valley_1987_with_Dashaka.jpg" decoding="async" width="150" height="167" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Jamiat_e-Islami_in_Shultan_Valley_1987_with_Dashaka.jpg/225px-Jamiat_e-Islami_in_Shultan_Valley_1987_with_Dashaka.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Jamiat_e-Islami_in_Shultan_Valley_1987_with_Dashaka.jpg/300px-Jamiat_e-Islami_in_Shultan_Valley_1987_with_Dashaka.jpg 2x" data-file-width="720" data-file-height="801" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><a href="/wiki/Hezb-i_Islami_Khalis" title="Hezb-i Islami Khalis">Hezb-i Islami Khalis</a> fighters in the Sultan Valley of <a href="/wiki/Kunar_Province" title="Kunar Province">Kunar Province</a>, 1987</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>In April 1978, the <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Democratic_Party_of_Afghanistan" title="People&#39;s Democratic Party of Afghanistan">People's Democratic Party of Afghanistan</a> (PDPA) seized power in the <a href="/wiki/Saur_Revolution" title="Saur Revolution">Saur Revolution</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="Coup d&#39;état">coup d'état</a> against then-President <a href="/wiki/Mohammed_Daoud_Khan" title="Mohammed Daoud Khan">Mohammed Daoud Khan</a>. The PDPA declared the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_Afghanistan" title="Democratic Republic of Afghanistan">Democratic Republic of Afghanistan</a>, with its first President named as <a href="/wiki/Nur_Muhammad_Taraki" title="Nur Muhammad Taraki">Nur Muhammad Taraki</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEwans2002186-88_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEwans2002186-88-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Opposition to PDPA reforms, such as its land redistribution policy and modernization of civil and marriage laws, led to unrest which became an open revolt by October 1978, first in eastern Afghanistan. That uprising quickly expanded into a <a href="/wiki/War_in_Afghanistan_(1978%E2%80%93present)" class="mw-redirect" title="War in Afghanistan (1978–present)">civil war</a> waged by guerrilla <a href="/wiki/Mujahideen" title="Mujahideen">mujahideen</a> against regime forces countrywide. The Pakistani government provided these rebels with covert training centers, while the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> sent thousands of military advisers to support the PDPA regime.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
The United States <a href="/wiki/Operation_Cyclone" title="Operation Cyclone">supported</a> Afghan <i>mujahideen</i> and foreign "<a href="/wiki/Afghan_Arab" class="mw-redirect" title="Afghan Arab">Afghan Arab</a>" fighters through Pakistan's <a href="/wiki/Inter-Services_Intelligence" title="Inter-Services Intelligence">Inter-Services Intelligence</a> (ISI).<sup id="cite_ref-Meher_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meher-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Meanwhile, increasing friction between the competing factions of the PDPA&#160;— the dominant <a href="/wiki/Khalq" title="Khalq">Khalq</a> and the more moderate <a href="/wiki/Parcham" title="Parcham">Parcham</a>&#160;— resulted (in July–August 1979) in the dismissal of Parchami cabinet members and the arrest of Parchami military officers under the pretext of a Parchami coup.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In September 1979, President Taraki was assassinated in a coup within the PDPA orchestrated by fellow Khalq member <a href="/wiki/Hafizullah_Amin" title="Hafizullah Amin">Hafizullah Amin</a>, who assumed the presidency. The situation in the country deteriorated under Amin and thousands of people went missing.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> The Soviet Union was displeased with Amin's government and decided to intervene and invade the country on 27 December 1979, killing Amin that same day.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarfield2012234_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarfield2012234-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A Soviet-organized regime, led by Parcham's <a href="/wiki/Babrak_Karmal" title="Babrak Karmal">Babrak Karmal</a> but inclusive of both factions (Parcham and Khalq), filled the vacuum. Soviet troops in more substantial numbers were deployed to stabilize Afghanistan under Karmal, marking the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Soviet%E2%80%93Afghan_War" title="Soviet–Afghan War">Soviet–Afghan War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> The United States continued to support the mujahideen through Pakistan's ISI<sup id="cite_ref-Meher_72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meher-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a>, delivering billions of dollars in cash and weapons including two thousand <a href="/wiki/FIM-92_Stinger" title="FIM-92 Stinger">FIM-92 Stinger</a> <a href="/wiki/Surface-to-air_missiles" class="mw-redirect" title="Surface-to-air missiles">surface-to-air missiles</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Brunei_Times_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Brunei_Times-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-The_Nation_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Nation-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The war lasted until 1989. Soviet forces, their <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_Afghanistan" title="Democratic Republic of Afghanistan">Afghan proxies</a> and rebels killed between 562,000<sup id="cite_ref-562k_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-562k-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> and 2 million Afghans,<sup id="cite_ref-Kakar_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kakar-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Reisman_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reisman-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Soviet-war-video_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Soviet-war-video-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-landmines_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-landmines-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> and displaced about 6 million people who subsequently fled Afghanistan, mainly to <a href="/wiki/Afghans_in_Pakistan" title="Afghans in Pakistan">Pakistan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Afghans_in_Iran" title="Afghans in Iran">Iran</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> Many countryside villages were bombed and some cities such as <a href="/wiki/Herat" title="Herat">Herat</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kandahar" title="Kandahar">Kandahar</a> were also damaged from air bombardment. Pakistan's <a href="/wiki/Khyber_Pakhtunkhwa" title="Khyber Pakhtunkhwa">North-West Frontier Province</a> functioned as an organisational and networking base for the anti-Soviet Afghan resistance, with the province's influential <a href="/wiki/Deobandi" title="Deobandi">Deobandi</a> ulama playing a major supporting role in promoting the 'jihad'.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Faced with mounting international pressure and numerous casualties, the Soviets <a href="/wiki/Soviet_withdrawal_from_Afghanistan" title="Soviet withdrawal from Afghanistan">withdrew from Afghanistan</a> in 1989, but continued to support Afghan President <a href="/wiki/Mohammad_Najibullah" title="Mohammad Najibullah">Mohammad Najibullah</a> until 1992.<sup id="cite_ref-Columbia:Afghanistan:History_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Columbia:Afghanistan:History-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Proxy_and_civil_war_and_Islamic_jihad_1989.E2.80.9396"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Proxy_and_civil_war_and_Islamic_jihad_1989–96">Proxy and civil war and Islamic jihad 1989–96</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Afghan_Civil_War_(1989%E2%80%9392)" class="mw-redirect" title="Afghan Civil War (1989–92)">Afghan Civil War (1989–92)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Afghan_Civil_War_(1992%E2%80%9396)" class="mw-redirect" title="Afghan Civil War (1992–96)">Afghan Civil War (1992–96)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:War_in_Afganistan_(1992%E2%80%932001).png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/War_in_Afganistan_%281992%E2%80%932001%29.png/220px-War_in_Afganistan_%281992%E2%80%932001%29.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="178" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/War_in_Afganistan_%281992%E2%80%932001%29.png/330px-War_in_Afganistan_%281992%E2%80%932001%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/War_in_Afganistan_%281992%E2%80%932001%29.png/440px-War_in_Afganistan_%281992%E2%80%932001%29.png 2x" data-file-width="1489" data-file-height="1207" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:War_in_Afganistan_(1992%E2%80%932001).png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Development of the civil war from 1992 to late 2001</div></div></div>
<p>After the Soviet withdrawal, the conflict between the mujahideen and the PDPA continued.<sup id="cite_ref-Stenersen_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stenersen-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>  President Najibullah, who had become president in 1987, tried to build support for his government by moving away from socialism to pan-Afghan <a href="/wiki/Nationalism" title="Nationalism">nationalism</a> and portraying his government as <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islamic</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarfield2012239,_244_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarfield2012239,_244-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Nevertheless, Najibullah did not win any significant support. In March 1989, mujahideen groups launched an attack on <a href="/wiki/Jalalabad" title="Jalalabad">Jalalabad</a>, instigated by the Pakistani ISI, but the attack failed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarfield2012241_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarfield2012241-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> With the <a href="/wiki/Dissolution_of_the_Soviet_Union" title="Dissolution of the Soviet Union">dissolution of the Soviet Union</a> in December 1991 and the ending of <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russian</a> support, President Najibullah was left without foreign aid. In March 1991, mujahideen forces attacked and conquered the city of <a href="/wiki/Khost" title="Khost">Khost</a>.
</p><p>In March 1992, President Najibullah agreed to step aside and make way for a mujahideen coalition government. At this time there were seven main mujahideen groups: <a href="/wiki/Hezb-e_Islami_Gulbuddin" title="Hezb-e Islami Gulbuddin">Hezb-e Islami (Gulbuddin faction)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hezb-i_Islami_Khalis" title="Hezb-i Islami Khalis">Hezb-e Islami (Khalis faction)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jamiat-e_Islami" title="Jamiat-e Islami">Jamiat-e Islami</a>, <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Dawah_Organisation_of_Afghanistan" title="Islamic Dawah Organisation of Afghanistan">Islamic Dawah Organisation of Afghanistan</a>, the <a href="/wiki/National_Islamic_Front_(Afghanistan)" class="mw-redirect" title="National Islamic Front (Afghanistan)">National Islamic Front for Afghanistan</a>, the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Jebh-e-Nejat-e_Melli&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Jebh-e-Nejat-e Melli (page does not exist)">National Liberation Front</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Harakat-i-Inqilab-i-Islami" class="mw-redirect" title="Harakat-i-Inqilab-i-Islami">Islamic Revolution Movement</a> . Their leaders <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Unity_of_Afghanistan_Mujahideen" title="Islamic Unity of Afghanistan Mujahideen">came together</a> in <a href="/wiki/Peshawar" title="Peshawar">Peshawar</a>, Pakistan, to negotiate a coalition government, but Hezbi Islami's leader <a href="/wiki/Gulbuddin_Hekmatyar" title="Gulbuddin Hekmatyar">Gulbuddin Hekmatyar</a> refused to confer and instead invaded Kabul. This kicked off a civil war, starting 25 April 1992, between initially three, but within weeks five or six mujahideen groups.<sup id="cite_ref-Amin_Saikal_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Amin_Saikal-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Human_Rights_Watch_(4)_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Human_Rights_Watch_(4)-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Roy_Gutman_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roy_Gutman-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> Kabul was heavily bombarded and partially destroyed by the fighting.<sup id="cite_ref-Human_Rights_Watch_(5)_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Human_Rights_Watch_(5)-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
