<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kabul_during_civial_war_of_fundamentalists_1993-2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Kabul_during_civial_war_of_fundamentalists_1993-2.jpg/220px-Kabul_during_civial_war_of_fundamentalists_1993-2.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="145" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Kabul_during_civial_war_of_fundamentalists_1993-2.jpg/330px-Kabul_during_civial_war_of_fundamentalists_1993-2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Kabul_during_civial_war_of_fundamentalists_1993-2.jpg/440px-Kabul_during_civial_war_of_fundamentalists_1993-2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="994" data-file-height="657" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kabul_during_civial_war_of_fundamentalists_1993-2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A section of Kabul during the <a href="/wiki/Afghan_Civil_War_(1992%E2%80%9396)" class="mw-redirect" title="Afghan Civil War (1992–96)">civil war</a> in 1993, which caused significant damage to the capital</div></div></div>
<p>As the war continued in 1993–95, the mujahideen committed widespread rape, murder and extortion.<sup id="cite_ref-Human_Rights_Watch_(4)_94-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Human_Rights_Watch_(4)-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Afghanistan_Justice_Project_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Afghanistan_Justice_Project-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Human_Rights_Watch_(5)_96-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Human_Rights_Watch_(5)-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> In January–June 1994, 25,000 people died in Kabul due to fighting between an alliance of <a href="/wiki/Abdul_Rashid_Dostum" title="Abdul Rashid Dostum">Abdul Rashid Dostum</a>'s <a href="/wiki/National_Islamic_Movement_of_Afghanistan" title="National Islamic Movement of Afghanistan">Junbish</a> with Hekmatyar's Hezbi Islami against <a href="/wiki/Ahmad_Shah_Massoud" title="Ahmad Shah Massoud">Ahmad Shah Massoud</a>'s Jamiat forces.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>
The <a href="/wiki/Taliban" title="Taliban">Taliban</a> emerged in September 1994 as a movement and militia of <a href="/wiki/Pashtuns" title="Pashtuns">Pashtun</a> students (<i>talib</i>) from Islamic <a href="/wiki/Madrassas_in_Pakistan" title="Madrassas in Pakistan">madrassas (schools) in Pakistan</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Human_Rights_Watch_(5)_96-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Human_Rights_Watch_(5)-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Matinuddin,_Kamal_1999_pp.25_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Matinuddin,_Kamal_1999_pp.25-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>
pledged to rid Afghanistan of 'warlords and criminals',<sup id="cite_ref-Stanford_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stanford-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>
and soon had military support from Pakistan.<sup id="cite_ref-George_Washington_University_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-George_Washington_University-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> In November 1994 the Taliban took control of <a href="/wiki/Kandahar" title="Kandahar">Kandahar city</a> after forcing out local Pashtun
leaders.<sup id="cite_ref-Human_Rights_Watch_(5)_96-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Human_Rights_Watch_(5)-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>
The Taliban in early 1995 attempted to capture Kabul but were repelled by forces under Massoud. The Taliban grew stronger and in September 1996 attacked and occupied Kabul after Massoud and Hekmatyar had withdrawn their troops from the city.<sup id="cite_ref-IRB,_Can.,1997_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IRB,_Can.,1997-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Taliban_Emirate_and_Northern_Alliance">Taliban Emirate and Northern Alliance</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Afghan_Civil_War_(1996%E2%80%932001)" title="Afghan Civil War (1996–2001)">Afghan Civil War (1996–2001)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Emirate_of_Afghanistan" title="Islamic Emirate of Afghanistan">Islamic Emirate of Afghanistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taliban" title="Taliban">Taliban</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Northern_Alliance" title="Northern Alliance">Northern Alliance</a></div>
<p>In late September 1996, the Taliban, in control of Kabul and most of Afghanistan,<sup id="cite_ref-c-profile_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-c-profile-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>  proclaimed the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Emirate_of_Afghanistan" title="Islamic Emirate of Afghanistan">Islamic Emirate of Afghanistan</a>. The Taliban were condemned internationally for the harsh enforcement of their interpretation of Islamic <a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">sharia</a> law, which resulted in the brutal treatment of many Afghans, especially <a href="/wiki/Taliban_treatment_of_women" title="Taliban treatment of women">women</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Skain_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Skain-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>  During their rule, the Taliban and their allies committed massacres against Afghan civilians, denied UN food supplies to 160,000 starving civilians and conducted a policy of <a href="/wiki/Scorched_earth" title="Scorched earth">scorched earth</a>, burning vast areas of fertile land and destroying tens of thousands of homes.<sup id="cite_ref-Rashid2_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rashid2-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Newsday_2001_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Newsday_2001-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-papillonsartpalace.com_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-papillonsartpalace.com-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NPR_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NPR-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Kabul_(1992%E2%80%9396)" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of Kabul (1992–96)">After the fall of Kabul</a> to the Taliban, Massoud and Dostum formed the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Alliance" title="Northern Alliance">Northern Alliance</a>. The Taliban defeated Dostum's forces during the <a href="/wiki/Battles_of_Mazar-i-Sharif_(1997%E2%80%9398)" title="Battles of Mazar-i-Sharif (1997–98)">Battles of Mazar-i-Sharif (1997–98)</a>. Pakistan's Chief of Army Staff, <a href="/wiki/Pervez_Musharraf" title="Pervez Musharraf">Pervez Musharraf</a>, began sending thousands of Pakistanis to help the Taliban defeat the Northern Alliance.<sup id="cite_ref-Webster_University_Press_Book_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Webster_University_Press_Book-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-George_Washington_University_101-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-George_Washington_University-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-History_Commons_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-History_Commons-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Maley_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maley-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ahmed_Rashid/The_Telegraph_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ahmed_Rashid/The_Telegraph-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> From 1996 to 2001, the <a href="/wiki/Al-Qaeda" title="Al-Qaeda">al-Qaeda</a> network of <a href="/wiki/Osama_bin_Laden" title="Osama bin Laden">Osama bin Laden</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ayman_al-Zawahiri" title="Ayman al-Zawahiri">Ayman al-Zawahiri</a> was also operating inside Afghanistan.<sup id="cite_ref-CNN_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CNN-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> Around 400,000 Afghans died in internal conflicts between 1990 and 2001.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 9 September 2001, Massoud was assassinated by two Arab <a href="/wiki/Suicide_attack" title="Suicide attack">suicide attackers</a> in <a href="/wiki/Panjshir_province" class="mw-redirect" title="Panjshir province">Panjshir province</a>. Two days later, the <a href="/wiki/September_11_attacks" title="September 11 attacks">September 11 attacks</a> were carried out in the United States. The US government suspected Osama bin Laden as the perpetrator of the attacks, and demanded that the Taliban hand him over.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> The Taliban offered to hand over Bin Laden to a third country for trial, but not directly to the US. Washington refused that offer.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> Instead, the US launched the October 2001 <a href="/wiki/War_in_Afghanistan_(2001%E2%80%93present)" title="War in Afghanistan (2001–present)">Operation Enduring Freedom</a>. The majority of Afghans supported the American invasion of their country.<sup id="cite_ref-Rejects_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rejects-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Public_Opinion_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Public_Opinion-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> During the initial invasion, US and UK forces bombed al-Qaeda training camps. Working with the Northern Alliance, the US removed the Taliban from power.<sup id="cite_ref-NYTOct8_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYTOct8-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Recent_history_.282002.E2.80.93present.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Recent_history_(2002–present)">Recent history (2002–present)</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/War_in_Afghanistan_(2001%E2%80%93present)" title="War in Afghanistan (2001–present)">War in Afghanistan (2001–present)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taliban_insurgency" title="Taliban insurgency">Taliban insurgency</a>, <a href="/wiki/Civilian_casualties_in_the_war_in_Afghanistan_(2001%E2%80%93present)" title="Civilian casualties in the war in Afghanistan (2001–present)">Civilian casualties in the war in Afghanistan (2001–present)</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Corruption_in_Afghanistan" title="Corruption in Afghanistan">Corruption in Afghanistan</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hamid_Karzai_2006-09-26.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Hamid_Karzai_2006-09-26.jpg/200px-Hamid_Karzai_2006-09-26.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="180" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Hamid_Karzai_2006-09-26.jpg/300px-Hamid_Karzai_2006-09-26.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c2/Hamid_Karzai_2006-09-26.jpg 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="360" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hamid_Karzai_2006-09-26.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Hamid_Karzai" title="Hamid Karzai">Hamid Karzai</a> dominated Afghan politics after the Taliban's fall</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Afghan_history_from_2008-2011.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Afghan_history_from_2008-2011.jpg/250px-Afghan_history_from_2008-2011.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="202" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Afghan_history_from_2008-2011.jpg/375px-Afghan_history_from_2008-2011.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Afghan_history_from_2008-2011.jpg/500px-Afghan_history_from_2008-2011.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="827" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Afghan_history_from_2008-2011.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>From upper left, clockwise – Canadian troops in Kandahar; American president Barack Obama meets Afghan leader Hamid Karzai in March 2010; US Secretary of State Hillary Clinton with female politicians in Kabul in October 2011; An officer of the RAF explains a C-27 of the Afghan air force to 'Thunder Lab' students in July 2011</div></div></div>
<p>In December 2001, after the Taliban government was overthrown, the <a href="/wiki/Afghan_Interim_Administration" title="Afghan Interim Administration">Afghan Interim Administration</a> under <a href="/wiki/Hamid_Karzai" title="Hamid Karzai">Hamid Karzai</a> was formed. The <a href="/wiki/International_Security_Assistance_Force" title="International Security Assistance Force">International Security Assistance Force</a> (ISAF) was established by the <a href="/wiki/UN_Security_Council" class="mw-redirect" title="UN Security Council">UN Security Council</a> to help assist the <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_Hamid_Karzai" title="Presidency of Hamid Karzai">Karzai administration</a> and provide basic security.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> Taliban forces meanwhile began regrouping inside Pakistan, while more coalition troops entered Afghanistan and began rebuilding the war-torn country.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Shortly after their fall from power, the Taliban began an <a href="/wiki/Taliban_insurgency" title="Taliban insurgency">insurgency</a> to regain control of Afghanistan. Over the next decade, ISAF and <a href="/wiki/Afghan_National_Army" title="Afghan National Army">Afghan troops</a> led many offensives against the Taliban, but failed to fully defeat them. Afghanistan remains one of the poorest countries in the world due to a lack of foreign investment, <a href="/wiki/Corruption_in_Afghanistan" title="Corruption in Afghanistan">government corruption</a>, and the Taliban insurgency.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Meanwhile, the <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_Afghanistan" title="Politics of Afghanistan">Afghan government</a> was able to build some democratic structures, and the country changed its name to the Islamic Republic of Afghanistan. Attempts were made, often with the support of foreign donor countries, to improve the country's economy, healthcare, education, transport, and agriculture. ISAF forces also began to train the <a href="/wiki/Afghan_National_Security_Forces" title="Afghan National Security Forces">Afghan National Security Forces</a>.  Following 2002, nearly five million <a href="/wiki/Afghan_diaspora" title="Afghan diaspora">Afghans</a> were <a href="/wiki/Repatriation" title="Repatriation">repatriated</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Adelman2016_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Adelman2016-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>By 2009, a Taliban-led shadow government began to form in parts of the country.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> In 2010, President Karzai attempted to hold <a href="/wiki/Afghan_Peace_Jirga_2010" title="Afghan Peace Jirga 2010">peace negotiations</a> with the Taliban leaders, but the rebel group refused to attend until mid-2015 when the Taliban supreme leader finally decided to back the peace talks.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In September 2014 <a href="/wiki/Ashraf_Ghani" title="Ashraf Ghani">Ashraf Ghani</a> became President after the <a href="/wiki/Afghan_presidential_election,_2014" class="mw-redirect" title="Afghan presidential election, 2014">2014 presidential election</a> where for the first time in Afghanistan's history power was democratically transferred.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup>  On 28 December 2014, NATO formally ended ISAF combat operations in Afghanistan and transferred full security responsibility to the Afghan government. The NATO-led <a href="/wiki/Operation_Resolute_Support" class="mw-redirect" title="Operation Resolute Support">Operation Resolute Support</a> was formed the same day as a successor to ISAF.<sup id="cite_ref-2014APcessation_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2014APcessation-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Guardian28_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian28-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> Thousands of NATO troops remained in the country to train and advise Afghan government forces<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> and continue their fight against the Taliban.<sup id="cite_ref-soufangroup_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-soufangroup-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> It was estimated in 2015 that "about 147,000 people have been killed in the Afghanistan war since 2001. More than 38,000 of those killed have been civilians".<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup>  A report titled <i>Body Count</i> concluded that 106,000–170,000 civilians have been killed as a result of the fighting in Afghanistan at the hands of all parties to the conflict.<sup id="cite_ref-IPPNW_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IPPNW-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Afghanistan" title="Geography of Afghanistan">Geography of Afghanistan</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Koppen-Geiger_Map_AFG_present.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Koppen-Geiger_Map_AFG_present.svg/220px-Koppen-Geiger_Map_AFG_present.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="114" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Koppen-Geiger_Map_AFG_present.svg/330px-Koppen-Geiger_Map_AFG_present.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Koppen-Geiger_Map_AFG_present.svg/440px-Koppen-Geiger_Map_AFG_present.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1643" data-file-height="850" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Koppen-Geiger_Map_AFG_present.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification" title="Köppen climate classification">Köppen climate map</a> of Afghanistan<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Landlocked" class="mw-redirect" title="Landlocked">landlocked</a> mountainous country with plains in the north and southwest, Afghanistan is located within South Asia<sup id="cite_ref-South_Asia_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-South_Asia-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-UNdata_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UNdata-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> and Central Asia.<sup id="cite_ref-Britannica_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> The country's highest point is <a href="/wiki/Noshaq" title="Noshaq">Noshaq</a>, at 7,492&#160;m (24,580&#160;ft) above sea level.<sup id="cite_ref-Factbook_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Factbook-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> It has a <a href="/wiki/Continental_climate" title="Continental climate">continental climate</a> with harsh winters in the <a href="/wiki/Hazarajat" title="Hazarajat">central highlands</a>, the glaciated northeast (around <a href="/wiki/Nuristan" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuristan">Nuristan</a>), and the <a href="/wiki/Wakhan_Corridor" title="Wakhan Corridor">Wakhan Corridor</a>, where the average temperature in January is below −15&#160;°C (5&#160;°F), and hot summers in the low-lying areas of the <a href="/wiki/Sistan_Basin" title="Sistan Basin">Sistan Basin</a> of the southwest, the <a href="/wiki/Jalalabad" title="Jalalabad">Jalalabad</a> basin in the east, and the <a href="/wiki/Afghan_Turkestan" title="Afghan Turkestan">Turkestan</a> plains along the <a href="/wiki/Amu_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Amu River">Amu River</a> in the north, where temperatures average over 35&#160;°C (95&#160;°F) in July. The lowest point lies in <a href="/wiki/Jowzjan_Province" title="Jowzjan Province">Jowzjan Province</a> along the Amu River bank, at 258&#160;m (846&#160;ft) above sea level.<sup id="cite_ref-Factbook_5-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Factbook-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Geography_of_Afghanistan.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Geography_of_Afghanistan.jpg/280px-Geography_of_Afghanistan.jpg" decoding="async" width="280" height="189" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Geography_of_Afghanistan.jpg/420px-Geography_of_Afghanistan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Geography_of_Afghanistan.jpg/560px-Geography_of_Afghanistan.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="690" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Geography_of_Afghanistan.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Afghanistan" title="Geography of Afghanistan">Landscapes of Afghanistan</a>, from left to right: 1. <a href="/wiki/Band-e_Amir_National_Park" title="Band-e Amir National Park">Band-e Amir National Park</a>; 2. <a href="/wiki/Salang_Pass" title="Salang Pass">Salang Pass</a> in <a href="/wiki/Parwan_Province" title="Parwan Province">Parwan Province</a>; 3. <a href="/wiki/Korangal_Valley" title="Korangal Valley">Korangal Valley</a> in <a href="/wiki/Kunar_Province" title="Kunar Province">Kunar Province</a>; and 4. <a href="/wiki/Kajaki_Dam" title="Kajaki Dam">Kajaki Dam</a> in <a href="/wiki/Helmand_Province" title="Helmand Province">Helmand Province</a></div></div></div>
<p>Despite having numerous rivers and <a href="/wiki/List_of_dams_and_reservoirs_in_Afghanistan" title="List of dams and reservoirs in Afghanistan">reservoirs</a>, large parts of the country are dry. The <a href="/wiki/Endorheic_basin" title="Endorheic basin">endorheic</a> Sistan Basin is one of the driest regions in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> Afghanistan receives snow during the winter in the <a href="/wiki/Hindu_Kush" title="Hindu Kush">Hindu Kush</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pamir_Mountains" title="Pamir Mountains">Pamir Mountains</a>, and the melting snow in the spring season enters the <a href="/wiki/List_of_rivers_of_Afghanistan" title="List of rivers of Afghanistan">rivers, lakes, and streams</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> However, two-thirds of the country's water flows into the neighboring countries of <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Turkmenistan" title="Turkmenistan">Turkmenistan</a>. The state needs more than <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$2 billion</span> to rehabilitate its irrigation systems so that the water is properly managed.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The northeastern Hindu Kush <a href="/wiki/Mountain_range" title="Mountain range">mountain range</a>, in and around the <a href="/wiki/Badakhshan_Province" title="Badakhshan Province">Badakhshan Province</a> of Afghanistan, is in a <a href="/wiki/Natural_environment#Geological_activity" title="Natural environment">geologically active</a> area where earthquakes may occur almost every year.<sup id="cite_ref-crone2007_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-crone2007-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> They can be deadly and destructive, causing <a href="/wiki/Landslide" title="Landslide">landslides</a> in some parts or <a href="/wiki/2009_Afghan_avalanches" title="2009 Afghan avalanches">avalanches</a> during the winter.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> The last strong earthquakes were in <a href="/wiki/February_1998_Afghanistan_earthquake" title="February 1998 Afghanistan earthquake">1998</a>, which killed about 6,000 people in Badakhshan near Tajikistan.<sup id="cite_ref-bbcquake2010_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbcquake2010-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> This was followed by the <a href="/wiki/2002_Hindu_Kush_earthquakes" title="2002 Hindu Kush earthquakes">2002 Hindu Kush earthquakes</a> in which over 150 people were killed and over 1,000 injured. A <a href="/wiki/2010_Afghanistan_earthquake" title="2010 Afghanistan earthquake">2010 earthquake</a> left 11 Afghans dead, over 70 injured, and more than 2,000 houses destroyed.
</p><p>The country's natural resources include: <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">coal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iron_ore" title="Iron ore">iron ore</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lithium" title="Lithium">lithium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">uranium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rare_earth_element" class="mw-redirect" title="Rare earth element">rare earth elements</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chromite" title="Chromite">chromite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gold" title="Gold">gold</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a>, <a href="/wiki/Talc" title="Talc">talc</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barite" class="mw-redirect" title="Barite">barite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marble" title="Marble">marble</a>, precious and <a href="/wiki/Semi-precious_stones" class="mw-redirect" title="Semi-precious stones">semi-precious stones</a>, <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">petroleum</a>, among other things.<sup id="cite_ref-peters2007_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-peters2007-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-bgs_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bgs-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> In 2010, US and Afghan government officials estimated that untapped mineral deposits located in 2007 by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Geological_Survey" title="United States Geological Survey">US Geological Survey</a> are worth at least <span class="nowrap">$1 trillion</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-bbcminerals_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbcminerals-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Afghanistan_physical_en.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Afghanistan_physical_en.png/220px-Afghanistan_physical_en.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="172" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Afghanistan_physical_en.png/330px-Afghanistan_physical_en.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Afghanistan_physical_en.png/440px-Afghanistan_physical_en.png 2x" data-file-width="2079" data-file-height="1624" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Afghanistan_physical_en.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Topography</div></div></div>
<p>At over 652,230&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (251,830&#160;sq&#160;mi),<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> Afghanistan is the world's <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_and_dependencies_by_area" title="List of countries and dependencies by area">41st largest country</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> slightly bigger than France and smaller than Burma, about the size of Texas in the United States. It borders Pakistan in the south and east; Iran in the west; Turkmenistan, Uzbekistan, and Tajikistan in the north; and China in the far east.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span></h2>

<p>The <a href="/wiki/Population" title="Population">population</a> of Afghanistan was estimated at 32.2 million in 2019. Of this, 16.4 million are males and 15.8 million females. About 23.9% of them are <a href="/wiki/Urban_area" title="Urban area">urbanite</a>, 71.4% live in rural areas, and the remaining 4.7% are nomadic.<sup id="cite_ref-official_population_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-official_population-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> An additional 3 million or so Afghans are temporarily housed in neighboring <a href="/wiki/Afghans_in_Pakistan" title="Afghans in Pakistan">Pakistan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Afghans_in_Iran" title="Afghans in Iran">Iran</a>, most of whom were born and raised in those two countries. The current population growth rate is 2.37%,<sup id="cite_ref-Factbook_5-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Factbook-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> the highest in the world outside of <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>. This population is expected to reach 82 million by 2050 if current population trends continue.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The only city with over a million residents is its capital, Kabul.  After Kabul the other five large cities are <a href="/wiki/Kandahar" title="Kandahar">Kandahar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Herat" title="Herat">Herat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mazar-i-Sharif" title="Mazar-i-Sharif">Mazar-i-Sharif</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kunduz" title="Kunduz">Kunduz</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jalalabad" title="Jalalabad">Jalalabad</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-official_population_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-official_population-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethnic_groups">Ethnic groups</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_groups_in_Afghanistan" title="Ethnic groups in Afghanistan">Ethnic groups in Afghanistan</a></div>
<p>Afghanistan's population is divided into several <a href="/wiki/Ethnolinguistics" title="Ethnolinguistics">ethnolinguistic</a> groups, which are represented in the ethnolinguistic map and listed in the chart below.
</p>
<div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:482px;"><a href="/wiki/File:US_Army_ethnolinguistic_map_of_Afghanistan_--_circa_2001-09.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/US_Army_ethnolinguistic_map_of_Afghanistan_--_circa_2001-09.jpg/480px-US_Army_ethnolinguistic_map_of_Afghanistan_--_circa_2001-09.jpg" decoding="async" width="480" height="439" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/US_Army_ethnolinguistic_map_of_Afghanistan_--_circa_2001-09.jpg/720px-US_Army_ethnolinguistic_map_of_Afghanistan_--_circa_2001-09.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/US_Army_ethnolinguistic_map_of_Afghanistan_--_circa_2001-09.jpg/960px-US_Army_ethnolinguistic_map_of_Afghanistan_--_circa_2001-09.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2319" data-file-height="2123" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:US_Army_ethnolinguistic_map_of_Afghanistan_--_circa_2001-09.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ethnolinguistics" title="Ethnolinguistics">Ethnolinguistic</a> groups of Afghanistan as of 2001</div></div></div></div>

</tr>

</tbody></table>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Dari_language" title="Dari language">Dari</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pashto" title="Pashto">Pashto</a> are the <a href="/wiki/Official_language" title="Official language">official languages</a> of Afghanistan; <a href="/wiki/Bilingualism" class="mw-redirect" title="Bilingualism">bilingualism</a> is very common.<sup id="cite_ref-AO_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AO-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Dari, which is a variety of and mutually intelligible with <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a> (and very often called 'Farsi' by some Afghans like in <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>) functions as the <a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a> in Kabul as well as in much of the northern and northwestern parts of the country.<sup id="cite_ref-AO_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AO-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Pashto is the native tongue of the Pashtuns, although many of them are also fluent in Dari while some non-Pashtuns are fluent in Pashto.
</p><p>There are a number of smaller regional languages, they include <a href="/wiki/Uzbek_language" title="Uzbek language">Uzbek</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turkmen_language" title="Turkmen language">Turkmen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Balochi_language" title="Balochi language">Balochi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pashayi_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Pashayi language">Pashayi</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nuristani_languages" title="Nuristani languages">Nuristani</a>. Some Afghans are also fluent in <a href="/wiki/Urdu" title="Urdu">Urdu</a>, English, and other foreign languages.<sup id="cite_ref-Afghan2018_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Afghan2018-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<p>Thousands of Afghan <a href="/wiki/Sikhism_in_Afghanistan" title="Sikhism in Afghanistan">Sikhs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hinduism_in_Afghanistan" title="Hinduism in Afghanistan">Hindus</a> are also found in the major cities.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup> There was a small <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Jews_in_Afghanistan" title="History of the Jews in Afghanistan">Jewish community in Afghanistan</a> who had emigrated to <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a> and the United States by the end of the twentieth century; at least one Jew, <a href="/wiki/Zablon_Simintov" title="Zablon Simintov">Zablon Simintov</a>, remained.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_Afghanistan" title="Christianity in Afghanistan">Afghan Christians</a>, who number 500–8,000, practice their faith secretly due to intense societal opposition.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Governance">Governance</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_Afghanistan" title="Politics of Afghanistan">Politics of Afghanistan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Afghanistan" title="Constitution of Afghanistan">Constitution of Afghanistan</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:New_Afghan_Parliament_Building.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/New_Afghan_Parliament_Building.png/220px-New_Afghan_Parliament_Building.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="126" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/New_Afghan_Parliament_Building.png/330px-New_Afghan_Parliament_Building.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/New_Afghan_Parliament_Building.png/440px-New_Afghan_Parliament_Building.png 2x" data-file-width="586" data-file-height="335" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:New_Afghan_Parliament_Building.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/National_Assembly_of_Afghanistan" class="mw-redirect" title="National Assembly of Afghanistan">National Assembly of Afghanistan</a> in 2016</div></div></div>
<p>Afghanistan is an <a href="/wiki/Islamic_republic" title="Islamic republic">Islamic republic</a> consisting of three branches, the executive, legislative, and judicial. The nation is led by President <a href="/wiki/Ashraf_Ghani" title="Ashraf Ghani">Ashraf Ghani</a> with <a href="/wiki/Abdul_Rashid_Dostum" title="Abdul Rashid Dostum">Abdul Rashid Dostum</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sarwar_Danish" title="Sarwar Danish">Sarwar Danish</a> as vice presidents. <a href="/wiki/Abdullah_Abdullah" title="Abdullah Abdullah">Abdullah Abdullah</a> serves as the chief executive officer (CEO). The <a href="/wiki/National_Assembly_of_Afghanistan" class="mw-redirect" title="National Assembly of Afghanistan">National Assembly</a> is the legislature, a <a href="/wiki/Bicameralism" title="Bicameralism">bicameral</a> body having two chambers, the <a href="/wiki/House_of_the_People_(Afghanistan)" title="House of the People (Afghanistan)">House of the People</a> and the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Elders" class="mw-redirect" title="House of Elders">House of Elders</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Afghan_Supreme_Court" class="mw-redirect" title="Afghan Supreme Court">Supreme Court</a> is led by <a href="/wiki/Chief_Justice_of_Afghanistan" title="Chief Justice of Afghanistan">Chief Justice</a> <a href="/w/index.php?title=Said_Yusuf_Halem&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Said Yusuf Halem (page does not exist)">Said Yusuf Halem</a>, the former Deputy Minister of Justice for Legal Affairs.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to <a href="/wiki/Transparency_International" title="Transparency International">Transparency International</a>, Afghanistan remains in the top most corrupt countries list.<sup id="cite_ref-TI_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TI-174">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup> A January 2010 report published by the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Office_on_Drugs_and_Crime" title="United Nations Office on Drugs and Crime">United Nations Office on Drugs and Crime</a> revealed that bribery consumed an amount equal to 23% of the GDP of the nation.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Elections_and_parties">Elections and parties</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Elections_in_Afghanistan" title="Elections in Afghanistan">Elections in Afghanistan</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_political_parties_in_Afghanistan" title="List of political parties in Afghanistan">List of political parties in Afghanistan</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:President_Donald_J._Trump_and_President_Ashraf_Ghani_of_Afghanistan_at_the_United_Nations_General_Assembly_(36747065014).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/President_Donald_J._Trump_and_President_Ashraf_Ghani_of_Afghanistan_at_the_United_Nations_General_Assembly_%2836747065014%29.jpg/220px-President_Donald_J._Trump_and_President_Ashraf_Ghani_of_Afghanistan_at_the_United_Nations_General_Assembly_%2836747065014%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/President_Donald_J._Trump_and_President_Ashraf_Ghani_of_Afghanistan_at_the_United_Nations_General_Assembly_%2836747065014%29.jpg/330px-President_Donald_J._Trump_and_President_Ashraf_Ghani_of_Afghanistan_at_the_United_Nations_General_Assembly_%2836747065014%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/President_Donald_J._Trump_and_President_Ashraf_Ghani_of_Afghanistan_at_the_United_Nations_General_Assembly_%2836747065014%29.jpg/440px-President_Donald_J._Trump_and_President_Ashraf_Ghani_of_Afghanistan_at_the_United_Nations_General_Assembly_%2836747065014%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:President_Donald_J._Trump_and_President_Ashraf_Ghani_of_Afghanistan_at_the_United_Nations_General_Assembly_(36747065014).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>U.S. President <a href="/wiki/Donald_Trump" title="Donald Trump">Donald Trump</a> with president of Afghanistan <a href="/wiki/Ashraf_Ghani" title="Ashraf Ghani">Ashraf Ghani</a> in 2017.</div></div></div>
<p>One instrument of Afghan governance is the <i><a href="/wiki/Loya_jirga" title="Loya jirga">loya jirga</a></i> (grand assembly), a <a href="/wiki/Pashtuns" title="Pashtuns">Pashtun</a> consultative meeting that is mainly organized for choosing a new <a href="/wiki/Head_of_state" title="Head of state">head of state</a>, adopting a new constitution, or to settle national or regional issue such as war.<sup id="cite_ref-news.bbc.co.uk_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-news.bbc.co.uk-176">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup>  Loya jirgas have been held since at least 1747,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarfield2012295_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarfield2012295-177">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> with the most recent one occurring in 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Under the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Afghanistan" title="Constitution of Afghanistan">2004 constitution</a>, both presidential and parliamentary elections are to be held every five years.  However, due to the disputed <a href="/wiki/2014_Afghan_presidential_election" title="2014 Afghan presidential election">2014 presidential election</a>, the scheduled 2015 parliamentary elections were delayed until <a href="/wiki/2018_Afghan_parliamentary_election" title="2018 Afghan parliamentary election">2018</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup> Presidential elections use the <a href="/wiki/Two-round_system" title="Two-round system">two-round system</a>; if no candidate receives a majority of the vote in the first round, a second round will be held featuring the top two candidates.  Parliamentary elections have only one round and are based on the <a href="/wiki/Single_non-transferable_vote" title="Single non-transferable vote">single non-transferable vote</a> system, which allows some candidates to be elected with as little as one percent of the vote.<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Afghan_presidential_election,_2004" class="mw-redirect" title="Afghan presidential election, 2004">2004 Afghan presidential election</a> was relatively peaceful, in which Hamid Karzai won in the first round with 55.4% of the votes. However, the <a href="/wiki/Afghan_presidential_election,_2009" class="mw-redirect" title="Afghan presidential election, 2009">2009 presidential election</a> was characterized by lack of security, low voter turnout, and widespread electoral fraud, ending in Karzai's reelection.<sup id="cite_ref-Karzai_Gets_New_Term_as_Afghan_Runoff_is_Scrapped_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karzai_Gets_New_Term_as_Afghan_Runoff_is_Scrapped-181">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Afghan_presidential_election,_2014" class="mw-redirect" title="Afghan presidential election, 2014">2014 presidential election</a> ended with Ashraf Ghani winning by 56.44% of the votes.<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Presidential_Palace_-_panoramio_(7).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Presidential_Palace_-_panoramio_%287%29.jpg/200px-Presidential_Palace_-_panoramio_%287%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Presidential_Palace_-_panoramio_%287%29.jpg/300px-Presidential_Palace_-_panoramio_%287%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Presidential_Palace_-_panoramio_%287%29.jpg/400px-Presidential_Palace_-_panoramio_%287%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Presidential_Palace_-_panoramio_(7).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Presidential_Palace,_Kabul" class="mw-redirect" title="Presidential Palace, Kabul">Arg (Presidential Palace)</a></div></div></div>
<p>Political parties played a marginal role in post-2001 Afghan politics, in part due to Karzai's opposition to them.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarfield2012301_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarfield2012301-183">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup>  In the <a href="/wiki/Afghan_parliamentary_election,_2005" class="mw-redirect" title="Afghan parliamentary election, 2005">2005 parliamentary election</a>, the ballots did not show candidates' party affiliation, so the results were dictated by the personal prestige of the candidates.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarfield2012301_183-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarfield2012301-183">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup>  Among the elected officials were former mujahideen, <a href="/wiki/Islamic_fundamentalists" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamic fundamentalists">Islamic fundamentalists</a>, warlords, communists, <a href="/wiki/Reformists" class="mw-redirect" title="Reformists">reformists</a>, and several Taliban associates.<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> In the same period, Afghanistan became the 30th highest nation in terms of female representation in the National Assembly.<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup>  Parties became more influential after 2009, when a new law established more stringent requirements for party registration.<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup>  Nearly a hundred new parties were registered after the law came into effect,<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> and party activity increased in the 2014 elections, but party influence remained limited.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Administrative_divisions">Administrative divisions</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Provinces_of_Afghanistan" title="Provinces of Afghanistan">Provinces of Afghanistan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Districts_of_Afghanistan" title="Districts of Afghanistan">Districts of Afghanistan</a></div>
<p>Afghanistan is administratively divided into 34 provinces (<i><a href="/wiki/Wilayah" title="Wilayah">wilayats</a></i>).<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup> Each province is the size of a <a href="/wiki/County_(United_States)" title="County (United States)">U.S. county</a>, having a <a href="/wiki/Governor" title="Governor">governor</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Capital_city" title="Capital city">capital</a>. The country is further divided into nearly 400 provincial <a href="/wiki/Districts_of_Afghanistan" title="Districts of Afghanistan">districts</a>, each of which normally covers a city or several villages. Each district is represented by a district governor.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/List_of_current_governors_of_Afghanistan" class="mw-redirect" title="List of current governors of Afghanistan">provincial governors</a> are appointed by the <a href="/wiki/President_of_Afghanistan" title="President of Afghanistan">President of Afghanistan</a>, and the district governors are selected by the provincial governors.<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup> The provincial governors are representatives of the central government in Kabul and are responsible for all administrative and formal issues within their provinces. There are also provincial councils that are elected through direct and general elections for four years.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup> The functions of provincial councils are to take part in provincial development planning and to participate in the monitoring and appraisal of other provincial governance institutions.
</p><p>According to article 140 of the constitution and the presidential decree on electoral law, mayors of cities should be elected through free and direct elections for a four-year term. In practice however, mayors are appointed by the government.<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The following is a list of all the 34 provinces in alphabetical order:
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Afghanistan_provinces_numbered.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Afghanistan_provinces_numbered.png/350px-Afghanistan_provinces_numbered.png" decoding="async" width="350" height="269" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Afghanistan_provinces_numbered.png/525px-Afghanistan_provinces_numbered.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/44/Afghanistan_provinces_numbered.png 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="461" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Afghanistan_provinces_numbered.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Afghanistan is divided into <a href="/wiki/Provinces_of_Afghanistan" title="Provinces of Afghanistan">34 provinces</a>, which are further divided into a <a href="/wiki/Districts_of_Afghanistan" title="Districts of Afghanistan">number of districts</a></div></div></div>
